<div id="review_76034833" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/76034833" />
    <a title="Raymond" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2376034-raymond"><img alt="Raymond" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1245094477p2/2376034.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/76034833?book_show_action=true">Oct 28, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Raymond" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Raymond" href="/user/show/2376034-raymond">Raymond</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780743222945" href="/book/show/971207.At_Swim_Two_Boys">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer76034833" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7176969773162158079">Now this was a good book! It's not like any of the gay novels that I've read before, and believe me, I've read quite a few. At Swim, Two boys was a little tricky to read at first because of the language. The book takes place in Ireland, and so I assume that a lot of the words that I did not understand were not words ordinarily used in the English dictionary. But it was a joy to read, anyway. The writing style was impressive, and once you get in the swing of understanding the language, it becomes</span>
  <span id="freeText7176969773162158079" style="display:none">Now this was a good book! It's not like any of the gay novels that I've read before, and believe me, I've read quite a few. At Swim, Two boys was a little tricky to read at first because of the language. The book takes place in Ireland, and so I assume that a lot of the words that I did not understand were not words ordinarily used in the English dictionary. But it was a joy to read, anyway. The writing style was impressive, and once you get in the swing of understanding the language, it becomes very pleasurable to indulge in. <br /><br />I say this book is not like any other gay novel I've read because it isn't. There is a genuine story here, with so many more elements than just gay youth coming together. There was a real story here, and it was filled with a lot of sadness, but also comedy. Mr. Mack, a father that is both difficult to deal with, but also very loving, is interesting to read about. His son, Jim, can be considered the protagonist, but the author wrote in the eyes of several different characters, probably so as to keep the audience from getting bored. I try doing that a lot in my own novels, and I think it is very effective. <br /><br />What I would like to know is if any of the events that occured in this novel actually happened. i know that it is a work of fiction, but the setting is true enough- World War One in Ireland, with the Irish soldiers being shipped off to Ireland's mother country of Great Britain to join in the war against Germany. There are some uprisings involving the Volunteer militia, and there are plenty of secrets in Dublin of the Socialist party. Whatever the case, it would be beneficial to know whether or not Jamie O'Neill knew much about Ireland's history. Ha, of course, the author probably IS from Ireland, though the book was published in America, according to the title page. <br /><br />I recommend this book to anybody who is tired of gay novels with the same silly elements- teenage boys developing crushes, desiring some other boy, and then being abused by society and their parents for their lifestyle. Not that any of those are necessarily bad- I do enjoy them occasionally- but this novel was definitely a breath of fresh air for me. Something new. Not to mention that you'll feel pretty smart after finishing the book- it really can be difficult to understand at first!</span>
  <a data-text-id="7176969773162158079" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="agirlwithoutwings" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/31446390-agirlwithoutwings"><img alt="agirlwithoutwings" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1444837857p2/31446390.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

        <a title="agirlwithoutwings" class="user" itemprop="url" name="agirlwithoutwings" href="/user/show/31446390-agirlwithoutwings">agirlwithoutwings</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer16532925853545634873">This is written in Irish so it was difficult to read, I couldn’t understand many parts of it but surely you’ll know it is written beautifully. However, my problem with this book was not the language, but the point of view. <b>I can’t get use to the stream of consciousness narration,</b> I don’t like it when I’ve read some pages without knowing who is telling me what is happening. <br/><br/>I enjoyed the first part of the story, Doyler and Jim’s genuine friendship and love was beautiful and promising.   <span id="freeText16532925853545634873" style="display:none">This is written in Irish so it was difficult to read, I couldn’t understand many parts of it but surely you’ll know it is written beautifully. However, my problem with this book was not the language, but the point of view. <b>I can’t get use to the stream of consciousness narration,</b> I don’t like it when I’ve read some pages without knowing who is telling me what is happening. <br /><br />I enjoyed the first part of the story, Doyler and Jim’s genuine friendship and love was beautiful and promising. But regarding the length of the book, in the second part I was a bit bored and wished the plot could be more engaging and not depressing. <br /><br />The characterization was good and it was easy to feel for them. But, honestly I just cared for what was going to happen to the boys and not the others. And there is MacMurrough too, who was an interesting character to read. Actually I think he was the main character of the book, and the boys seemed to be symbols of desires for him, one sexual and the other more platonic. His presence was at times fun to read and sometimes frustrating too! IN A WAY THAT I WANTED THE BOOK MORE FOCUSED ON THE BOYS!<br /><br />This is a tragic love story and that made it even more difficult, the way these young people’s lives and fates were ravaged by politics, religion, beliefs, war, poverty and many other damn things wasn’t easy to read, my heart was ripped for Doyler’s life and at the end for Jim. Oh, I HATED the ending!<br /></span>

    <a title="Jason" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1257623-jason"><img alt="Jason" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/39044580?book_show_action=true">Dec 01, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jason" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jason" rel="nofollow" href="/user/show/1257623-jason">Jason</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer39044580" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17864897953180948135">While the language is a bit difficult to grasp - the author writes in a very distinct Irish syntax - you get used to it after the first 50 pages or so. It's absolutely worth getting through those first few pages to get to the heart of this beautifully constructed love story. It's a rare thing that I should connect so deeply with literary characters...I can only think of a few instances that I've experienced it. Jane Eyre, Jo March and Laurie, Kavalier and Clay all come to mind...and now, Jim and</span>
  <span id="freeText17864897953180948135" style="display:none">While the language is a bit difficult to grasp - the author writes in a very distinct Irish syntax - you get used to it after the first 50 pages or so. It's absolutely worth getting through those first few pages to get to the heart of this beautifully constructed love story. It's a rare thing that I should connect so deeply with literary characters...I can only think of a few instances that I've experienced it. Jane Eyre, Jo March and Laurie, Kavalier and Clay all come to mind...and now, Jim and Doyler. While there's a great deal of historical reference to keep you interested, what drew me into this novel was the intimate, beautiful relationship between the two boys and even their strange relationship with MacMurrough. Jim and Doyler's friendship blossoms throughout the novel into something that all of us dream of and yet rarely seem to find: absolute love. Their story made me ache for a romance story of my own. <br /><br />This is one of those stories that one wants to read in short increments to prolong the experience, yet one can't seem to read fast enough to find out the fate of the two lovers. I highly recommend it to anyone with an open mind and a receptive heart.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17864897953180948135" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2463922848" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2463922848" />
    <a title="randi-" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/79117826-randi-"><img alt="randi-" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1596142952p2/79117826.jpg" /></a>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/79117826-randi-?shelf=favourites">favourites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/79117826-randi-?shelf=lgbtq">lgbtq</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/79117826-randi-?shelf=good-ol-cry">good-ol-cry</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/79117826-randi-?shelf=5star">5star</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/79117826-randi-?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2463922848" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10046654853621686485">(5/5)<br/>At first I thought I’d have trouble while reading this book because of its heavy Irish language however, as each chapter crossed I found myself understanding more and more the way words change or take place and how easy metaphors seemed. <br/>This book was a master piece made by words, beautifully written and had a very genuine and realistic atmosphere that grabbed me from the first page. It was as if I was taken back in time to experience the war that held Irland at that time, and through readi</span>
  <span id="freeText10046654853621686485" style="display:none">(5/5)<br />At first I thought I’d have trouble while reading this book because of its heavy Irish language however, as each chapter crossed I found myself understanding more and more the way words change or take place and how easy metaphors seemed. <br />This book was a master piece made by words, beautifully written and had a very genuine and realistic atmosphere that grabbed me from the first page. It was as if I was taken back in time to experience the war that held Irland at that time, and through reading I learned so much of what had happened. To learn of historic events in such a way, of how certain people lived and how they survived in such times, from the poor to the rich, to soldiers and shop keepers, priests and educators, middle class citizens and others, all was described in such a raw and believable way that left me speechless at some points. <br />As the book tells the story of Jim and Doyler, of their simple yet profound friendship that grabs at your heart, of how each one faces certain obstacles in their lives and how the war changed them as boys, it was truly a privilege to go through their lives and know their thoughts and watch them trade stories, their boyish conversations and no fear of speaking their minds. Their love and trust for each other, and how no obstacle they faced could separate them was beautiful. <br />We read this story through many perspectives and each one holds a different story to be told. As the characters are introduced you get attached to so many of them and that was the biggest achievement of this book. Character development was very natural and took a heavy role on the story. <br />This book touches many topics in a way that feels very picturesque, very true and in a simple yet realistic way that engages the readers to think more, to open their eyes more to certain ideas/topics. These said topics include rape, socialism, democracy, citizenship, nationalism, war and social class. <br />It is very important to start this book with the knowledge of these topics being real and how times have changed since then, how it was dealt with before and how it is dealt with now. <br />At swim, two boys takes you for a ride that leaves you heartbroken at times yet with a full blown smile at others, heartwarming and true words encase you and words of wise men and politics consume you. <br />An extraordinary read and a very witty and astounding way to be written. </span>
  <a data-text-id="10046654853621686485" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3007135" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17344154922827941920">A classic love story with a few fine twists, set in 1915–1916 against the turbulence of Irish politics and society. Jamie O'Neill has been compared to both Joyce and Dickens (for his facility with language, and his comic characters). Discard any trashy notions of a "gay fiction" — think more of the austere aesthetic of "Chariots of Fire" or Mary Renalt's pathbreaking <i>The Charioteer.</i> Richly written, avoiding cliché, <i>At Swim</i> deserves all the hype it got when it was first published.<br/><br/></span>

          </span>
        </div>

(I first read it some years back when the book was released.) <br/>I was amazed of how much of the story did not make an impression then, but seemed to powerful now. <br/>It's a story of young budding love in beautiful Ireland, but all against a world revolution, the Catholic Church, class divisions and extreme poverty.<br/>And it's really a great story of pride, and saying love – for whomever – is okay and beautiful. <br/>I feel as though I've just come from a tr</span>
  <span id="freeText10850088374359954533" style="display:none">I just read this amazing novel for the second time. (I first read it some years back when the book was released.) <br />I was amazed of how much of the story did not make an impression then, but seemed to powerful now. <br />It's a story of young budding love in beautiful Ireland, but all against a world revolution, the Catholic Church, class divisions and extreme poverty.<br />And it's really a great story of pride, and saying love – for whomever – is okay and beautiful. <br />I feel as though I've just come from a trip to Ireland....<br /> </span>

          </span>
        </div>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('TC1iIvBwzv2adxj7PzVuxG1eSvNk8gk+H7KVwsy8Bt5Z1ht5ltEJg77hZuNYKj9aYspHi/ZkkGJwYF/WaV3Emw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('vOb4WXAVb09SPITPsOepg3tzJKuhMHbywRJnZpZWUZGpHYECFrSoMXaq+tfX+PgddOcp0zOm766uwK1yM7eT1A==')})
  }
//]]>

He dutifully plays the role of the good son for his meat-and-potatoes father, even as his own mind is a jumble of sexual confusion and painful self-doubt. But everything changes in one, horrifying instant when a tragic accident wakes his family from their middle-American dream and plunges them into a spiral of slow destruction.\n\nAs his family falls apart day by day, Robin finds himself pulling away from the unquestioned, unexamined life that has been carefully laid out for him. Small acts of rebellion lead to larger questions of what it means to stand on his own. Falling into a fevered triangle with two other outcasts, Todd Spicer and Scott Schatz, Robin embarks on an explosive odyssey of sexual self-discovery that will take him beyond the spring-green lawns of suburbia, beyond the fraying fabric barely holding together his quickly unraveling family, and into a complex future, beyond the world of normal boys.\n\nIn The World Of Normal Boys,  K.M. (New York magazine), a &quot;brilliant chronicler of the disconnect…between who we are and who we wish we might have been&quot; (Wall Street Journal), and a writer of &quot;fiction at its most supremely interesting&quot; (Colm Tóibín). \nNow, with his third and most ambitious novel, Aciman delivers an elegant and powerful tale of the wages of assimilation—a moving story of an immigrant’s remembered youth and the nearly forgotten costs and sacrifices of becoming an American.\n\nIt’s the fall of 1977, and amid the lovely, leafy streets of Cambridge a young Harvard graduate student, a Jew from Egypt, longs more than anything to become an assimilated American and a professor of literature. He spends his days in a pleasant blur of seventeenth-century fiction, but when he meets a brash, charismatic Arab cab driver in a Harvard Square café, everything changes.\n\nNicknamed Kalashnikov—Kalaj for short—for his machine-gun vitriol, the cab driver roars into the student’s life with his denunciations of the American obsession with &quot;all things jumbo and ersatz&quot;—Twinkies, monster television sets, all-you-can-eat buffets—and his outrageous declarations on love and the art of seduction. The student finds it hard to resist his new friend’s magnetism, and before long he begins to neglect his studies and live a double life: one in the rarified world of Harvard, the other as an exile with Kalaj on the streets of Cambridge. Together they carouse the bars and cafés around Harvard Square, trade intimate accounts of their love affairs, argue about the American dream, and skinny-dip in Walden Pond. But as final exams loom and Kalaj has his license revoked and is threatened with deportation, the student faces the decision of his life: whether to cling to his dream of New World assimilation or risk it all to defend his Old World friend.\n\nHarvard Square is a sexually charged and deeply American novel of identity and aspiration at odds. It is also an unforgettable, moving portrait of an unlikely friendship from one of the finest stylists of our time.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"12498909682757005890\" href=\"#\"       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/55328.Jamie_O_Neill">About Jamie O&#39;Neill</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/55328.Jamie_O_Neill"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1235980377p3/55328.jpg);'></div>

<a href="/author/show/55328.Jamie_O_Neill">Jamie O'Neill

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer58647567362043058">Jamie O'Neill is an Irish author, who lived and worked in England for two decades; he now lives in Gortachalla, in County Galway, Ireland. His critically-acclaimed novel, At Swim, Two Boys (2001) earned him the highest advance ever paid for an Irish novel and frequent claims that he was the natural successor to James Joyce, Flann O'Brien and Samuel Beckett.<br/><br/>O'Neill was born in Dún Laoghaire in 1962</span>
  <span id="freeText58647567362043058" style="display:none">Jamie O'Neill is an Irish author, who lived and worked in England for two decades; he now lives in Gortachalla, in County Galway, Ireland. His critically-acclaimed novel, At Swim, Two Boys (2001) earned him the highest advance ever paid for an Irish novel and frequent claims that he was the natural successor to James Joyce, Flann O'Brien and Samuel Beckett.<br /><br />O'Neill was born in Dún Laoghaire in 1962 and was educated at Presentation College, Glasthule, County Dublin, run by the Presentation Brothers, and (in his words) "the city streets of London, the beaches of Greece." He was raised in a home without books, and first discovered that books "could be fun" when he read Ivanhoe by Sir Walter Scott. O'Neill was unhappy at home; he had a very difficult relationship with his father and ran away from home at age 17.<br /><br />O'Neill was the partner of television presenter Russell Harty for six years until Harty's death in 1988. His current partner is Julien Joly, a former ballet dancer who now works as a Shiatsu therapist.<br /><br />O'Neill lists as his favourite books: Ulysses, by James Joyce, The Last of the Wine, by Mary Renault, Hadrian VII, by Fr. Rolfe (Frederick Baron Corvo), The History of the Decline and Fall of the Roman Empire, by Edward Gibbon, The Leopard, by Giuseppe Tomasi di Lampedusa, The Siege of Krishnapur, by J. G. Farrell, One Hundred Years of Solitude, by Gabriel Garcia Marquez, The Third Policeman, by Flann O'Brien, The Swimming-Pool Library, by Alan Hollinghurst, and The Lost Language of Cranes by David Leavitt.<br /><br />O’Neill met Russell Harty in 1982, during a two-week holiday in London. They became a couple and lived together in London and at Rose Cottage, Harty's home in Giggleswick, Yorkshire. Harty encouraged O'Neill's writing and read his manuscripts; he even mailed manuscripts of early novels to publishers without O'Neill's consent or knowledge, and a book deal was agreed with Weidenfeld. Soon after that, in 1988, Russell Harty died of AIDS-related Hepatitis B. Hounded by the tabloid press, O'Neill's nude photograph was splashed across the front of the Sunday Mirror; the picture was taken shortly after his arrival in London when he earned some money as a model. He turned down offers of up to £50,000 for interviews about his private life with Russell Harty.<br /><br />This newspaper coverage was how O'Neill's parents in Ireland discovered that their son was gay. This event would have been traumatising enough; his distress was deepened when members of the Harty family threw him out of the cottage, burned his clothes and left him homeless. They did, however, allow him to take the couple's pet dog, Paddy; even though they did want it.<br /><br />After Russell Harty's death, O'Neill sought therapeutic help. The following year, O'Neill's first novel, Disturbance, was published; Kilbrack followed in 1990. Both novels had been mostly finished while Harty was alive. But then, grieving for Harty and alone in London, O'Neill struggled to write, parted company with both his agent and publisher, and took the job as a night porter at the Cassell Hospital, a psychiatric institution in Surrey from 1990 up to 2000.<br /><br />Two years after Russell Harty's death, Paddy was to accidentally introduce O'Neill to his future partner. O'Neill was in a London pub when he noticed the dog was missing. Paddy had been found by a ballet dancer named Julien Joly. They began a relationship and Joly was instrumental in helping O'Neill put his life back together. During the ten years that followed, O'Neill wrote At Swim, Two Boys, which was published in 2001. Its official launch at Somerset House in London was abandoned on the day -- it was September 11, 2001.</span>
  <a data-text-id="58647567362043058" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/956105-at-swim-two-boys">Quotes from At Swim, Two Boys</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;I’m just thinking that would be pleasant. To be reading, say, out of a book, and you to come up and touch me – my neck, say, or my knee – and I’d carry on reading, I might let a smile, no more, wouldn’t lose my place on the page. It would be pleasant to come to that. We’d come so close, do you see, that I wouldn’t be surprised out of myself every time you touched.&rdquo;</span>

One day surely the wars would end, and Jim would come home, if only to lie broken in MacMurrough’s arms, he would come to his island home. And MacMurrough would have it built for him, brick by brick, washed by the rain and the reckless sea. In the living stream they’d swim a season. For maybe it was true that no man is an island: but he believed that two very well might be.&rdquo;</span>
